Title: A Parallel Evolutionary Algorithm Framework for Graph k-CUT Problems

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2607.17158v1

Published Time: Tue, 21 Jul 2026 01:02:37 GMT

Number of Pages: 88

Markdown Content:
# A Parallel Evolutionary Algorithm Framework for Graph 

# k-CUT Problems 

## Sihong Shao ∗

## , Chuan Yang †

Abstract 

Graph k-CUT problems include many important variants whose objectives combine cut value, volume, and cardinality terms in different ways. Most existing algorithms are designed for individual formulations, which limits their transferability across related models. In this paper, we organize a broad family of graph partitioning problems into two classes, MaxGCP and MinGCP, according to their optimization orientation and balance-related structure. Based on this classification, we propose a unified Parallel Evolutionary Algorithm Framework (PEAF). This framework combines structure-inheriting crossover operators, a hierarchical mutation mechanism based on the Multiple Mutation Heuristic (MMH) and the Auxiliary Cut Mutation Heuristic (ACMH), and a diversity-preserving selection strategy. Extensive experiments on G-set with k ∈ { 2, 3, 4, 5} show that PEAF-ACMH consistently outperforms Gurobi on nine representative k-CUT problems. For MaxGCP, PEAF-ACMH improves several best-known solutions for Max -k-Cut with 

k ≥ 3, and through numerical bounds derived from its relation to Max -k-Cut , ver-ifies the high quality of the obtained solutions for Judicious -k-Partition and An-tiCheeger -k-Cut . The results further indicate that Judicious -k-Partition usually yields more balanced partitions than AntiCheeger -k-Cut . For MinGCP, theoreti-cal and computational comparisons show that Cheeger -k-Cut and Sparsest -k-Cut 

produce more balanced partitions than Normalized -k-Cut and Ratio -k-Cut , respec-tively. PEAF-ACMH also obtains highly similar partitions for Min -k-Cut and Min-Max -k-Cut within short running times, providing numerical evidence for their struc-tural affinity. These results demonstrate that PEAF is both an effective unified solver and a useful tool for revealing structural properties of graph k-CUT models. 

Keywords: Evolutionary algorithm; Cheeger cut; Maxcut; Normalized cut; Mincut; Ratio cut; Minmax cut; Sparsest cut; Judicious partition; AntiCheeger cut 

AMS subject classifications: 68W50; 90C27; 05C85; 90C59; 68W10

. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 79 31 Introduction 

Graph k-CUT problems aim to partition the vertices of a graph into k (k ≥ 2) disjoint subsets by removing the edges among them. These problems encompass a variety of graph cut types, including Max -k-Cut [36], Judicious -k-Partition [5], AntiCheeger -k-Cut 

[60], Min -k-Cut [26], MinMax -k-Cut [13], Normalized -k-Cut [57], Ratio -k-Cut [19], 

Cheeger -k-Cut [41], and Sparsest -k-Cut [1, 10]. For simplicity, we categorize these prob-lems into two main groups: Maximal Graph Cut Problems (MaxGCP) and Minimal Graph Cut Problems (MinGCP) (see Definitions 1 and 2). The distinction is based on the preference for larger cuts in MaxGCP and smaller cuts in MinGCP. Both categories have garnered sig-nificant attention due to their wide-ranging applications. MaxGCP are particularly valuable in fields such as statistical physics [3, 40], VLSI circuit layout design [14, 17], and machine learning [58]. MinGCP are extensively applied in image segmentation [8, 9, 23], stereo vision [11, 35], clustering [10, 18, 53], community detection [50, 51], data classification [48], and data mining [33]. With the exception of Min -k-Cut [26] and MinMax -k-Cut [13] (for fixed k), the afore-mentioned nine graph k-cut problems are NP-hard [27, 32, 36, 47, 54, 55, 57]. For these NP-hard variants, exact algorithms are often restricted to small-scale instances due to their exponential computational complexity. For instance, the branch-and-bound approach pro-posed by Krislock et al. [37], despite incorporating quadratic regularization and quasi-Newton methods, is limited to Max -2-Cut on graphs with fewer than 500 vertices. Consequently, heuristic methods are indispensable for tackling large-scale sparse graphs encountered in real-world applications. Significant efforts have been directed toward developing robust heuristics for these challenging problems. Within the MaxGCP framework, diverse strategies have been proposed for Max -2-Cut , ranging from tabu search [2] and scatter search [45] to GRASP, variable neighborhood search (VNS), and path-relinking [24]. Other notable approaches in-clude simple iterative algorithms [56] and rank-two relaxation heuristics [12]. Similarly, for MinGCP variants such as Ratio -k-Cut and Normalized -k-Cut , Palubeckis [52] introduced multistart simulated annealing and memetic algorithms. Furthermore, recent advancements for Cheeger -2-Cut feature breakout local search [43], hybrid evolutionary algorithms [42], and parallel Markov Chain Monte Carlo methods [15]. Research on heuristic algorithms for various graph cut problems has been disproportion-ate, leaving a notable gap between theoretical investigation and algorithmic development. For instance, while Max -k-Cut has been extensively studied, numerical results for Judicious -k-

Partition remain scarce despite decades of combinatorial interest [5, 6, 34, 38]. To bridge this gap and establish a unified computational framework, we propose an efficient parallel evolutionary algorithm framework for generalized k-cut problems. Our approach addresses variables defined by boundaries (2.4), volumes (2.5), and cardinalities (2.6), which are sys-tematically categorized into MaxGCP and MinGCP (see Section 2). The primary contributions of this work are summarized as follows: • A High-Performance Multiple Mutation Heuristic (MMH): We develop MMH (see Algorithm 2 and Section 3.3) as a versatile search engine for generalized k-Cut 

problems. MMH integrates nine search operators organized into three hierarchies: local search ( ˜O1, ˜O2,̂ O1,̂ O2), tabu search ( ˜O3, ˜O4,̂ O3,̂ O4), and strong random perturbation (O5) (see Sections 3.3.1, 3.3.2 and 3.3.3). The efficiency is underpinned by a well-designed 4bucket sorting structure that maintains two stable move-gain matrices (see Eqs. (3.10) and (3.13)), enabling rapid tracking of changes in boundaries (2.4) and cut values (2.3). Furthermore, we establish the computational complexities (Proposition 1) and provide quality guarantees specifically for Max -k-Cut and Min -k-Cut (Proposition 2). • An Enhanced Auxiliary Cut Mutation Heuristic (ACMH): To improve search robustness, we propose ACMH (see Section 3.3.4 and Algorithm 3), an advanced variant of MMH. ACMH adaptively rotates between the primary objective and strategically se-lected auxiliary cut problems. This multi-tasking mutation strategy facilitates escaping local optima by observing whether superior solutions to the primary problem emerge from the landscape of auxiliary tasks. • A Synergized Parallel Evolutionary Framework (PEAF): We integrate the pro-posed heuristics into the mutation phase in PEAF (see Algorithm 1), resulting in two algorithms: PEAF-MMH and PEAF-ACMH. This framework coordinates three distinct crossover mechanisms (see Section 3.2) involving five operators: C1 utilizes a scoring-based inheritance (Section 3.2.1); C2,1 and C2,2 employ greedy subgraph reconstruction (Section 3.2.2); and C3,1, C 3,2 implement path-relinking to explore mutual paths be-tween reference solutions (Section 3.2.3). Finally, a solution pool selection mechanism (Section 3.4 and Algorithm 4). These three phases work together to inherit shared struc-tural characteristics from parent solutions while maintaining a robust balance between population quality and genetic diversity. • Extensive Benchmarking and Structural Discovery: Comprehensive experiments on G-set ∗ for nine k-cut variants ( k ∈ { 2, 3, 4, 5}) demonstrate that PEAF-ACMH consis-tently outperforms the Gurobi solver. For MaxGCP, PEAF-ACMH improves a number of best-known solutions for Max -k-Cut with k ≥ 3 reported in [44]. More impor-tantly, by using these best-known Max -k-Cut values as reference results and carefully exploiting the close relationships among Max -k-Cut , Judicious -k-Partition , and 

AntiCheeger -k-Cut , we derive numerical bounds (Eqs. (5.1) and (5.4)) that validate the high quality of the solutions obtained for the latter two problems (Tables 4 and 10). These results further show that Judicious -k-Partition usually produces more balanced partitions than AntiCheeger -k-Cut (Section 5.2). For MinGCP, our theo-retical and computational analyses show that Cheeger -k-Cut and Sparsest -k-Cut 

tend to produce more balanced partitions than Normalized -k-Cut and Ratio -k-Cut ,respectively (Sections 6.2 and 6.6). We also observe that PEAF-ACMH obtains very similar partitions for Min -k-Cut and MinMax -k-Cut , while solving both problems within very short running times. This provides numerical evidence for the structural affinity between the two problems. It is also consistent with the known complexity land-scape: Min -k-Cut has long been known to be polynomial-time solvable [26], whereas the corresponding complexity of MinMax -k-Cut was settled only recently [13]. The rest of this paper is organized as follows. Section 2 introduces the mathematical for-mulations and notations for the nine graph k-CUT problems. Section 3 details the proposed parallel evolutionary algorithm framework, including crossover, mutation and selection mech-anisms. In Section 4, we conduct extensive numerical experiments on the G-set and analyze 

> ∗Downloaded from https://web.stanford.edu/ ∼yyye/yyye/Gset/

5the results. Finally, Section 7 concludes the paper and discusses potential avenues for future research. 

# 2 Graph k-CUT problems 

Given an undirected graph G = ( V, E ), V = [ n] := {1, . . . , n } and E ⊂ V × V are the sets of vertices and edges, respectively. Each edge {u, v } ∈ E is associated with a positive integer weight, denoted as wuv ∈ Z+, and du = ∑ 

> {u,v }∈ E

wuv represents the degree of the u-th vertex. For a nonempty subset S ⊂ V , vol( S) sums the degrees of all vertices in S and |S| gives the number of vertices contained in S. Let S be a family of nonempty subsets of V and E(S)

collect all boundaries among these subsets 

E(S) = {{ u, v } ∈ E : u ∈ I, v ∈ J, ∀ I̸ = J, I ∈ S, J ∈ S} . (2.1) Accordingly, we define the boundary of S, ∂S = E({S, S c}), with Sc being the complement of S in V , and 

cut( ∂S ) = ∑

> {u,v }∈ ∂S

wuv . (2.2) We call E(S) a k-CUT of G if S = {S1, . . . , S k} partitions V into k (≥ 2) disjoint nonempty subsets, and the corresponding cut value is 

cut( S) = ∑

> {u,v }∈ E(S)

wuv , (2.3) which happens to be the objective function for both Max -k-Cut (2.9) and Min -k-Cut (2.12). For any partition S = {S1, . . . , S k}, we call its subset Sp a cut segment. Moreover, the set of all k-CUTs of G is denoted as E. For the partition S = {S1, . . . , S k} of V , we make the following conventions 

cut( ∂S) = {cut( ∂S 1), cut( ∂S 2), . . . , cut( ∂S k)}, (2.4) 

vol( S) = {vol( S1), vol( S2), . . . , vol( Sk)}, (2.5) 

|S| = {| S1|, |S2|, . . . , |Sk|} . (2.6) The objective functions of nine graph k-CUT problems studied in this paper are all composites of the aforementioned three set-valued functions towards the partition S = {S1, S 2, . . . , S k} of 

V and can be rewritten into the following abstract form 

F (cut( ∂S), vol( S), |S|) , (2.7) with the corresponding optimization problems being 

opt 

> S∈E

F (cut( ∂S), vol( S), |S|), opt ∈ { min , max }. (2.8) To be more specific, the nine graph k-CUT problems can be formulated as follows.

(2.9)-(2.17) that the nine objective functions all con-tain the set-valued function cut( ∂S) which directly characterizes how closely the partitioned vertex subsets corresponds to each other. Using this fact, we would like to categorize those nine graph cut problems into two types, for which we replace cut( ∂S) with x = ( x1, x 2, . . . , x k) ∈ Rk

to extend the discrete function F from E to R into a continuous function ¯F from Rk to R:

¯F (x) := F (x, vol( S), |S|).

Furthermore, we are able to define the partial derivative of ¯F at the point x = ( x1, x 2, . . . , x k) ∈

Rk with respect to the p-th variable xp

∇p ¯F = lim 

> h→0

¯F (. . . , x p−1, x p + h, x p+1 . . . ) − ¯F (. . . , x p−1, x p, x p+1 . . . )

h , ∀ p ∈ [k]. (2.18) Finally, the aforementioned nine problems can be categorized into two types: Maximal Graph Cut Problems (MaxGCP) and Minimal Graph Cut Problems (MinGCP). 

Definition 1. A k-CUT problem belongs to MaxGCP if either ∇p ¯F ≤ 0, ∀ x ∈ Rk, ∀ p ∈ [k]

holds for opt = min ; or ∇p ¯F ≥ 0, ∀ x ∈ Rk, ∀ p ∈ [k] holds for opt = max .

Definition 2. A k-CUT problem belongs to MinGCP if either ∇p ¯F ≥ 0, ∀ x ∈ Rk, ∀ p ∈ [k]

holds for opt = min ; or ∇p ¯F ≤ 0, ∀ x ∈ Rk, ∀ p ∈ [k] holds for opt = max .

It is easy to verify that the first three cut problems in Eqs. (2.9)-(2.11) pertain to MaxGCP, and the remaining six in Eqs. (2.12)-(2.17) belong to MinGCP. That is, no matter the bal-anced indicators — volume (2.5) and cardinality (2.6) show up or not in the objective func-tions, the basic pursuit of MaxGCP is to find solutions with large cut values (2.3) and close 7boundary (2.4) connections, whereas MinGCP favors solutions with small cut values and loose boundary connections. Considering Normalized -k-Cut (2.14) as an example, we have 

opt = min , ¯F (x) = ∑kp=1  

> xp
> vol( Sp)

and ∇p ¯F ≥ 0, ∀ x ∈ Rk, ∀ p ∈ [k], suggesting that minimizing 

¯F requires smaller values of x. That implies the objective function (2.7) and the boundary sizes (2.4) either increase or decrease simultaneously, meaning that reduced values of cut( ∂S)

are more likely to yield improved objective function values for Normalized -k-Cut .

# 3 Parallel evolutionary algorithm framework 

This paper presents a parallel evolutionary algorithm framework PEAF for solving the graph k-CUT problems that satisfy opt F ∈ { MaxGCP , MinGCP }. The primary objective of this framework is to maintain a population of high-quality solutions. Initially, PEA randomly selects pairs of individuals from the population and applies multiple crossover operators to exchange and combine their excellent features. Along this line, various mutation operators are employed to explore new regions of the solution space, thereby facilitating the potential discovery of improved solutions. Both the crossover and mutation processes are executed in parallel to enhance the computational efficiency. The formation of the new population involves selecting solutions from the union of the original population and the newly generated offspring, which ensures that the new population is not only of high quality but also of great diversity. The oveall procedure is outlined in Algorithm 1. 

Algorithm 1 A parallel evolutionary algorithm framework (PEAF) for opt F on G = ( V, E ).                                           

> Require: The population size m, the number of possible solution pairs r, the time limit for stopping.
> Ensure: An approximate solution I∗to the graph k-CUT problem opt Fon G
> 1: Initialization :I←generate_population (G, m )▷Section 3.1
> 2: while the stopping condition is not satisfied do
> 3: C←parallel_random_crossover (I, r )▷Section 3.2
> 4: M←parallel_mutation (C)▷Section 3.3
> 5: I←update_population (M∪I)▷Section 3.4
> 6: end while
> 7: I∗←best (I)
> 8: return I∗

The initial population I, whose generation is detailed in Section 3.1, comprises m = |I|

solutions, and each individual is a partition of V . Algorithm 1 iterates repeatedly until a specified time limit is reached and outputs the best solution I∗ in the final solution pool. In each iteration, we first randomly pick r pairs of reference solutions from the current population, and each pair is applied with a randomly selected crossover operator (see Section 3.2) to generate two offspring solutions that preserve the common excellent traits. These r runs are executed in parallel and output the offspring solution set C with |C| = 2 r. Each solution of 

C is input to the mutation phase (see Section 3.3) to pursue deep improvement. All these 2r

runs are also executed in parallel and output the offspring solution set M with |M| = 2 r. The new population for the next iteration is selected from M ∪ I, the merged set of the existing solution pool and M, as detailed in Section 3.4. 83.1 Initial population 

We adopt the following random initialization procedure: k different vertices are randomly selected and allocated separately to k subsets (thus empty subsets are avoided), then each of the remaining vertices is randomly assigned to one of those k subsets. A special care should be taken sometimes for the isolated vertices in MinGCP. For example, if the number of isolated vertices is no less than k in Min -k-Cut , MinMax -k-Cut , Ratio -k-Cut , and Sparsest -k-

Cut , it will result in a trivial solution with the objective function value being 0. Thus, an additional step should be included to examine the number of isolated vertices. 

## 3.2 Crossover 

In order to combine shared traits from the reference (parent) solutions, one usually adopts some crossover operators to produce offspring solutions. Before that, it is crucial to establish a one-to-one mapping among the cut segments of the reference solutions to identify the shared component, which is defined to be the induced subgraph of G on the shared vertices that appear in the same labeled cut segments. Specifically, for any two reference solutions, I1 =

{S11 , S 12 , . . . , S 1 

> k

} and I2 = {S21 , S 22 , . . . , S 2 

> k

}, with one-to-one mapping S2 

> p

→ S1 

> p

> p

∩ S2 

> p

) representing the union of the shared vertices and forms the backbone of I1 and I2. The shared component G[V0]

is considered the combination of advantageous features (i.e. the shared vertices and their connecting edges) from I1 and I2, and should be preserved in the offspring solutions, denoted by ¯I1 and ¯I2. This implies that the segment labels of the shared vertices remain unchanged in the offspring solutions, namely, 

lab( v, ¯I1) = lab( v, ¯I2) = lab( v, I 1) = lab( v, I 2), ∀ v ∈ V0 = ∪kp=1 (S1 

> p

∩ S2 

> p

), (3.1) where the segment label of the vertex v for any solution I = {S1, . . . , S k} is denoted as 

lab( v, I ), which is equal to p if v ∈ Sp. Therefore, the major role of any crossover operator is to determine the segment labels of the remaining vertices V c 

> 0

in the offspring solutions. The first task is to establish the one-to-one mapping among the cut segments of the ref-erence solutions. In order to enlarge the shared component as wide as possible, we tend to maximize the number of the shared vertices in a greedy manner, borrowing the idea in the greedy partition crossover operators utilized in graph segmentation [4, 59] and graph coloring [21, 25, 31]. Here are the specific steps. Firstly, compute a matrix S ∈ Nk×k with each element being Sij = |S1 

> i

∩ S2 

> j

|. Secondly, match a position pair by (i∗, j ∗) ∈ arg max ij {S ij }, ignore the 

i∗-th row and j∗-th column and repeat this step on the remaining part of S until all k pairs are established. Thirdly, for each pair (i, j ), rearrange the j-th cut segment S2 

> j

in I2 to be the i-th segment S2 

> i

and make the mapping S2 

> i

→ S1 

> i

3.2.1 Crossover operator C1

The operator C1, originally designed for Max -2-Cut [45], is now extended to address the general k-CUT problem “ opt F ”, via scoring the reference solutions, I1, I2, and the segment labels of that with a higher score are more more likely being inherited. Assuming without loss of generality that F (I1) ≥ F (I2), we record the score for I1 as 

score 1 =

F (I1)

F (I1) + F (I2), if opt = max ,F (I2)

F (I1) + F (I2), if opt = min ,

and then the score for I2 is 1 − score 1. In consequence, each vertex v in ¯I1 is assigned the segment label lab( v, ¯I1) according to 

{lab( v, I 1), if r ≤ score 1,

lab( v, I 2), if r > score 1, (3.2) where r is randomly selected from a uniform distribution U[0 , 1] . On the other hand, each segment label of ¯I2 is obtained from fixing r = 0 .5 in Eq. (3.2), thus ¯I2 happens to be the better between I1 and I2.

3.2.2 Crossover operators C2,1 and C2,2

We extend the combination operator CB 2 proposed in [45] to form two novel crossover operators, C2,1 and C2,2, tailored for general problem “ opt F ”. Both start from the shared component G[V0] and proceed by greedily assigning the remaining vertices to the cut segments iteratively until recovering to the original graph G. ## 3.3 Mutation 

For any opt F in MinGCP and MaxGCP, we design a multiple mutation heuristic (MMH) (see Algorithm 2) to enhance the solution quality after the crossover phase, inspired by the multiple operator heuristic (MOH) for Max -k-Cut (2.9) [44]. MMH incorporates four local search (see ˜O1,̂ O1, ˜O2,̂ O2), four tabu search (see ˜O3, ˜O4,̂ O3,̂ O4) and one strong random perturbation (see O5) operators, whereas MOH employs five search operators. A basic search operator in MOH is the single-transfer O1, a best-of-all Variable Neighborhood Search (VNS) operator to seek the simplest move that yields the largest change (see Eq. (3.5)) in the cut value function (see Eq. (2.3)) [28, 49]. To be more specific, after inputting a solution I =

{S1, . . . , S k}, O1 examines the natural move-gain matrix D (3.7) of “ F = 12

∑kp=1 cut( ∂S p)” to make the best simplest move v → Sq to form I′ = I ◦ (v → Sq) that maximizes F (I′) within the first order neighborhood I′ ∈ E(1) (I), and subsequently updates D for the next move. The majority of elements in D remain unchanged and only those related to the vertices in {v}∪N (v)

need to be recalculated after O1 with N (v) = {u ∈ V : {u, v } ∈ E} being the neighborhood of 

v. Consequently, the number of elements requiring updates is O(kd max ), where dmax denotes the highest degree of the graph, enabling O1 to be a rapid search operator. Nevertheless, the best-of-all search rule may not be effective for other k-CUT problems, especially for those with objective functions involving vol( S) or |S|. Taking Normalized -k-Cut (2.14) as an example, the number of elements in D that necessitates updating after O1 is O(k|V |), making the computation time-consuming [30]. To strike a balance between the solution quality and computational cost, several reduced VNS methods [28–30, 44] have been implemented, such as substituting the best-of-all rule with the first-of-all rule or utilizing random search strategies. In a similar way, MMH replaces the best-of-all rule with a “best-of-part” strategy to form ˜O1

and ̂ O1 as detailed in Section 3.3.1. Each time applying ˜O1 and ̂ O1 involves updating the move-gain matrices ˜D and ̂ D, respectively, and the number of elements requiring updates are both O(kd max ). Here are the definitions of ˜D and ̂ D.

The cut stable move-gain matrix ˜D Given any solution I = {S1, S 2, . . . , S k}, let the change in the cut value function (2.3) be 

˜∆M = cut( I ◦ M ) − cut( I), (3.9) when applying a move M , and then define 

˜D = ( ˜∆v→Sq )v∈V, q ∈[k] ∈ Zn×k. (3.10) 13 A direct calculations shows ˜D = ( ˜Dvq )n×k with

The double-transfer local search operators ˜O2 and ̂ O2 moves an edge, i.e., two simplest moves, to improve F and are triggered when ˜O1 and ̂ O1 exhaust, respectively, (see Algorithm 2, lines 4-18 and Section 3.3.1). The computational cost of these four local search operators are lower than those of best-of-all rules (see Proposition 1) with the benefit of bucket sorting. When they fail to enhance the objective function value, MMH switches to the tabu search operators ˜O3, ˜O4,̂ O3,̂ O4 (see Algorithm 2, lines 26-41 and Section 3.3.2). Upon reaching the maximum perturbation or surpassing the objective function value, MMH reverts to the local search phase. If the consecutive ξ rounds of local-tabu search fail to improve the solution quality, a strong random perturbation operator O5 is applied (see Algorithm 2, line 43 and Section 3.3.3). The termination condition for mutation can be either the time limit or the maximum number of iterations. Following MMH, we propose a category search operator to further enhance the solution quality via applying MMH into an auxiliary graph k-CUT problem of the same category and the resulting algorithm is called an auxiliary cut mutation heuristic (ACMH). It is motivated by some early attempts using Max -2-Cut to generate high-quality solutions for AntiCheeger -

2-Cut and Max -Bisection [12, 55]. In some sense, ACMH aims to explore high-quality solutions in the neighborhood of the auxiliary cut approximations. 14 Algorithm 2 A multiple mutation heuristic (MMH) on graph G = ( V, E ).

3.3.1 Local search operators ˜O1,̂ O1, ˜O2,̂ O2

The best-of-part rule utilizes the move-gain matrix to seek improved solutions within lim-ited yet promising search areas, as opposed to the best-of-all rule which exhaustively explores all possible solutions in the neighborhoods. The proposed four local search operators fully follow the best-of-part rule. For simplicity, we denote the move-gain matrix D = ( Dvq )n×k

In a word, ˜O1 or ̂ O1 selects the best simplest move within D(I), a subset of all possible moves in the first order neighborhood of the current solution I.

0 0 0 0 0Two comments follow accordingly. • Utilizing the bucket sorting schemes [44] to store the stable move-gain matrices, the search area D(I), whose size |D(I)| is notably smaller than k|V | in general cases, is easily obtained as detailed in Appendix A. The update of each bucket sorting scheme when acting a simplest move is efficient with the computational cost being O(kd max ) instead of O(k|V |). The cost analysis of ˜O1,̂ O1, ˜O2 and ̂ O2 is delineated in Proposition 1. • For Max -k-Cut and Min -k-Cut , Proposition 2 demonstrates that the best-of-part search operators ˜O1 and ˜O2 are nothing but the best-of-all ones in the first and second order neighborhoods, respectively. 

Proposition 1. The computational costs of the local search operators are bounded as follows: 1. For ˜O1 and ̂ O1, the complexity is at most O(max {k|D(I)|, kd max }).2. For ˜O2 and ̂ O2, the complexity is at most O(kd max |D(I)|).Proof. Please see Appendix A. 

Proposition 2. Given any unweighted graph and “ F = 12

∑kp=1 cut( ∂S p)”, the operators ˜O1

and ˜O2 are able to identify local optima in the first and second order neighborhoods, respectively. 

17 Proof. Min -k-Cut shares the similar proof with Max -k-Cut that we assume “ opt = max ”in the followings. If ˜O1 can not improve the cut value for the current solution I, ˜Dvq ≤ 0

holds for ∀ (v, q ) ∈ D(I) (see Eq. (3.17)), thus ˜Dvq ≤ 0 holds for ∀ v ∈ V and ∀ q ∈ [k], leading to I being the local optimum in the first order neighborhood. Next we prove that if there exists a double-transfer move Spu → u → Squ and Spv → v → Sqv that can improve the cut value, i.e. ˜∆(u,v ) > 0, ˜O2 will promote the cut value. Since the current solution I is a local maximum in E(1) (I), we have ˜∆u→Squ ≤ 0 and ˜∆v→Sqv ≤ 0, indicating ˜ϕw uv = 2 to satisfy 

˜∆(u,v ) > 0 and at least one of ˜∆u→Squ = 0 and ˜∆v→Sqv = 0 must be satisfied, implying that either (v, q v) or (u, q u) belongs to D(I). Therefore, this double-transfer move Spu → u → Squ

and Spv → v → Sqv is within the search area of ˜O2.

3.3.2 Tabu search operators ˜O3, ˜O4,̂ O3,̂ O4

When above four local search operators fail to enhance the quality of the current solution, we turn to the tabu search phase, inspired by the approach in [44]. This phase integrates the local search operators with a tabu list L, the i-th element in which represents the tabu tenure 

Li of the vertex i, implying that the vertex i is prohibited from being moved in the next Li

iterations. The single-transfer tabu search operators ˜O3 and ̂ O3 are derived from ˜O1 and ̂ O1, respec-tively, while the double-transfer tabu search operators ˜O4 and ̂ O4 originate from ˜O2 and ̂ O2,respectively. Before detailing the implementation of the tabu search operators, we define the 

j-th search layer of the bucket sorting storage scheme for any solution I as

L, D1(I, L ) = D(I) (see Eq. (3.17)) exactly represents the search area of the corresponding single-transfer local search operator. 

The single-transfer tabu search operator ˜O3 or ̂ O3 determines the vertex v∗ and the moved in subset Sq∗ layer-by-layer in a first-of-all strategy. To begin with j = 1 , the move is randomly selected from (v∗, q ∗) ∈ Dj (I, L ), and set j ← j + 1 if Dj (I, L ) = ∅, otherwise stop the search. 

The double-transfer tabu search operator ˜O4 or ̂ O4 utilizes neighborhoods of Dj (I, L )

∅, ∀ v ∈ Dj (I, L ) and we set j ← j + 1 .Above four tabu search operators are designed to discover improved solutions in the vicinity of those generated by the local search operators. When these tabu search operators fail to provide enough diversity to continue the improvement, MMH switches to a strong perturbation operator. 

3.3.3 Random perturbation operator O5

The strong perturbation operator O5 randomly selects η vertices and moves each se-lected vertex to a different segment, where η is sampled by randn(0 .1|V |, 0.3|V |) (Algorithm 2, lines 43–44). The resulting η single-vertex moves impose a substantial perturbation on the cur-rent partition, which enhances diversity and enables the algorithm to explore distant regions of the search space. After O5, MMH reverts to the local search phase. 

3.3.4 Category search: An auxiliary cut mutation heuristic (ACMH) 

ACMH combines the target graph cut problem with several auxiliary ones by reusing MMH under different objective functions. Since MMH takes the objective function as an input, the same search procedure can be applied to different graph cut formulations without changing its main structure. ACMH first applies MMH to the target objective, and then uses auxiliary objectives to guide the search toward different regions of the solution space. During the auxiliary search, each newly updated solution is still checked against the target objective, so that any improvement for the target problem can be retained. The detailed procedure is outlined in Algorithm 3. 

Algorithm 3 An auxiliary cut mutation heuristic (ACMH) on G = ( V, E ).

Require: 

opt F (·) : target k-CUT problem ;opt F1(·), . . . , opt Fp(·) : p different auxiliary k-CUT problems ;

I∗ = {S1, . . . , S k} : initial solution ;

nmax : maximum number of search operations.  

> 1:

while not satsfying stopping criteria do  

> 2:

Input I∗ and opt F (·) to MMH in Algorithm 2 and then output I∗ when the number of search operations reach nmax . 

> 3:

I ← I∗. 

> 4:

Randomly select i ∈ [p].

19 5: Input I and opt Fi(·) to MMH in Algorithm 2 with a monitor: Add the following lines 

If F (I) is better than F (I∗) then 

I∗ ← I. 

end if 

after each update I ← I ◦ O in Algorithm 2 with O ∈ { ˜O1,̂ O1, ˜O2,̂ O2, ˜O3,̂ O3, ˜O4,̂ O4, O 5}.Output I∗ when the number of search operations reach nmax . 

> 6:

end while  

> 7:

return I∗.

Note that the auxiliary k-CUT problems should belong to the same category (MaxGCP or MinGCP) as the target, although they may differ in balance. Numerical experiments in Section 4 shows that ACMH achieves superior solution quality compared to MMH for e.g., 

Normalized -2-Cut (see Table 23). 

## 3.4 Population selection 

A possible solution set P = I ∪ M is composed of I, the current population with size being 

|I| = m and M, the solutions after mutation with size being |M| = 2 r (see Algorithm 1, line 6). Instead of exclusively selecting the top m best solutions in P as the new population, we select the top m/ 2 best solutions in P to constitute half of the population, denoted as 

P1. Such selection ensures the quality of the new population. The remaining half of the population, denoted by P2, is obtained by solving the following Maximum Diversity Problem (MDP) [22, 46] that introduces diversity to the new population, 

P2 = arg max 

> P2⊂P\P1,|P2|=m/ 2

diversity (P1 ∪ P2),

where the diversity function is defined for any p solutions {It}pt=1 as follows diversity ({It}pt=1 ) = ∑

> 1≤i<j ≤p

dist C (Ii, I j ).

Here the distance metric dist C (Ii, I j ) counts the number of edges located in exactly one of the solutions Ii and Ij [45]. Algorithm 4 displays the entire procedure for obtaining the new polulation from the set P where a greedy local search method is adopted to solve MDP. 

Algorithm 4 Population selection from the possible solution set P for opt F (·).

Require: m + 2 r : number of solutions in P. 

> 1:

Sort P in ascending order of the objective function values F if opt = min , while sort P in descending order of F if opt = max . 

> 2:

P1 = {It}m/ 2 

> t=1

, P2 = {It}mt=m/ 2+1 and P3 = {It}m+2 rt=m+1 . 

> 3:

while diversity (P1 ∪ P2) can be improved do  

> 4:

Swap the solution Ii ∈ P2 and Ij ∈ P3 when diversity (P1 ∪ P2) increases the most.  

> 5:

end while  

> 6:

return P1 ∪ P2.

20 4 Experiment settings 

We conduct numerical experiments for all 9 graph k-CUT problems with k = 2 , 3, 4, 5 in Section 2 on 35 graphs with non-negative weights from the well-known benchmark G-set. Both MMH in Algorithm 2 and ACMH in Algorithm 3 are integrated into PEAF in Algorithm 1, and the resulting algorithms are dubbed PEAF-MMH and PEAF-ACMH, respectively. We set the population size m = 8 in Algorithm 4 and the number of possible solution pairs r = 4 

in Algorithm 1. The stopping criterion is a predefined runtime limit: 



15 minutes , if opt F ∈ { Min -k-Cut , MinMax -k-Cut },



30 minutes , if |V | < 5000 ,

120 minutes , if 5000 ≤ | V | < 10000 ,

240 minutes , if |V | ≥ 10000 ,

otherwise. (4.1) In view of that MOH itself is an efficient algorithm to solve Max -k-Cut [44], we also con-duct numerical experiments utilizing the mutation-only algorithms MMH and ACMH to solve generalized graph k-CUT problems. That is, we will make a performance comparison among PEAF-MMH, PEAF-ACMH, MMH and ACMH on the same footing, all of which are compiled using GNU G++ with compiling flags “ -pthread -O2 ” on the computing platform of 2*Intel Xeon E5-2650-v4/2.2GHz with 128GB RAM. For all of them, we select the tabu tenure Li for vertex i by 

Li ∈ randn(3 , L max ), Lmax ∈ randn (0 .05 |V |, 0.15 |V |) , (4.2) where randn( a, b ) denotes a random integer between a and b. Other remaining parameters are detailed in Table 2. The auxiliary cuts needed in ACMH and PEAF-ACMH are listed in Table 3. We use Gurobi (version 10) as a reference solver under two settings, G-H and G-0.5H. Both settings use Gurobi’s NoRel heuristic, which searches for feasible solutions before the root-node relaxation. G-H sets NoRelHeurTime=timeLimit , allowing this heuristic to use the whole time budget, while G-0.5H sets NoRelHeurTime=0.5*timeLimit , leaving the remaining time for Gurobi’s standard MIP search. We run G-H and G-0.5H on the platform of AMD TR1950X/3.4GHz with 128GB RAM under the same runtime limits in Eq. (4.1). 

# 5 Numerical results for MaxGCP 

This section presents numerical results of PEAF-ACMH, PEAF-MMH, ACMH, MMH, G-H and G-0.5H in solving three k-CUT problems in MaxGCP: Judicious -k-Partition (2.10) (see Section 5.1), AntiCheeger -k-Cut (2.11) (see Section 5.2) and Max -k-Cut (2.9) (see Section 5.3). For each graph, we report the average objective function value of top 4 solutions, as only half of the population give the highest-quality solutions. For the best solution, the columns best, cut, and time report the objective function value, the corresponding cut value, and the elapsed time in seconds when the best solution is first found, respectively. A smaller time means that the algorithm reaches its best result earlier. Specifically, the best objective value among all those six algorithms is highlighted in bold. Gurobi on all graph instances, with the only exception of G70 when k = 2 . 29 5.2 AntiCheeger-k-Cut 

Tables 11, 12, 13 and 14 display the numerical results for AntiCheeger -k-Cut with 

k = 2 , 3, 4, 5. PEAF-ACMH, PEAF-MMH, ACMH, and MMH outperform Gurobi on all graph instances, except for G70 at k = 2 . The pairwise comparisons involving PEAF-ACMH in Table 15 shows that it outperforms the other three algorithms, and the comparison between PEAF-MMH and MMH gives that the parallel evolutionary framework indeed improves the solution quality. Using a similar analysis in Section 5.1, we are able to show that PEAF-ACMH achieves high-quality solutions for AntiCheeger -k-Cut on G-set. For each graph instance G = ( V, E )

and k ∈ { 2, 3, 4, 5}, let aco (G, k ) and acc (G, k ) be the best objective function value (e.g. column 3 of Table 11) and the corresponding cut value (e.g. column 4 of Table 11) generated by PEAF-ACMH, respectively. Thus, we have 

2mc (G, k )vol( V ) ≈ 2Max-k-Cut (G)vol( V ) ≥ AntiCheeger-k-Cut (G) ≥ aco (G, k ),

2mc (G, k )vol( V ) ≈ 2Max-k-Cut (G)vol( V ) ≥ 2acc (G, k )vol( V ) ≥ aco (G, k ),

(5.4) and using the lower bound over the upper bound leads to ratio 1(G, k ) = vol( V )aco (G, k )2mc (G, k ) , (5.5) ratio 2(G, k ) = acc (G, k )

mc (G, k ) . (5.6) The two ratios assess the PEAF-ACMH solution from two aspects. The first ratio com-pares the obtained AntiCheeger -k-Cut objective function value with the reference bound 

2mc (G, k )/vol( V ). The second ratio compares the cut value of the same solution with the reference Max -k-Cut value mc (G, k ). Thus, when both ratios are close to 1, the obtained solution is close to the reference bound in terms of the AntiCheeger -k-Cut objective and also has a cut value close to the best-known Max -k-Cut result. Since mc (G, k ) is used as a proxy for Max -k-Cut (G), these ratios are interpreted as numerical quality indicators. Accordingly, we can draw the conclusion that PEAF-ACMH achieves high-quality solutions through the numerical values presented in Table 10. Table 10 shows that the PEAF-ACMH solutions are very close to the reference bound 2mc (G, k )/vol( V ) for AntiCheeger -k-Cut :all instances satisfy |ratio 1 − 1| < 0.05 for every k = 2 , 3, 4, 5. The worst value of ratio 2 is at least 0.99819, indicating that the cut values of the obtained solutions almost match the best-known Max -k-Cut values. When ratio 2 = 1 , the solution attains the same cut value as the reference Max -k-Cut result while further optimizing the balance-oriented objective. 

Balance analysis It can be numerically observed that Judicious -k-Partition behaves more balanced than AntiCheeger -k-Cut since the latter exhibits 71 solutions with larger cut values, whereas the former only yields 19 larger cut values, out of 140 best solutions produced by PEAF-ACMH. That is, AntiCheeger -k-Cut tends to have larger cut value and is closer to Max -k-Cut , thereby producing more unbalanced cuts, than Judicious -k-

Partition . This can be also verified using a similar analysis proposed in [20] for k = 2 .30 Table 10: Summary of the two quality ratios in Eqs. (5.5)–(5.6) for AntiCheeger -k-Cut on G-set. From above observations, we are able to see that numerical solutions of high-quality indeed help to transparently validate the combinatorial characteristics, such as the degree of balance discussed here, which may be easily neglected by those of low-quality. 31 Table 11: Numerical results for  AntiCheeger -2-Cut  on G-set.

36 5.3 Max-k-Cut 

Tables 18, 19, 20 and 21 respectively record the numerical results for Max -k-Cut with 

k = 2 , 3, 4, 5. PEAF-ACMH, PEAF-MMH, ACMH, and MMH outperform Gurobi on all graph instances, except for G70 at k = 2 . G70 is extremely sparse, with 10000 vertices and 9999 edges. For k = 3 , 4, 5, both Gurobi and our algorithms find partitions with no internal edges for Max -k-Cut , Judicious -k-Partition , and AntiCheeger -k-Cut . Hence, every edge is cut and the partition induces k independent sets. The result on G70 may therefore reflect the special sparsity of this instance, rather than a general advantage of Gurobi . In Table 22, we compare the results of ACMH and MMH, observing that MMH consistently outperforms ACMH, particularly for k > 2. This may be attributed to the fact that the move-gain matrix 

˜D directly stores the changes in the objective function values for Max -k-Cut , obviating the necessity for the auxiliary cut. For each graph instance G = ( V, E ) and k ∈ { 2, 3, 4, 5}, let mc 1(G, k ) be the best cut value (e.g. the best of Columns 3, 7, 11 and 15 in Table 18), and we calculate ratio 0(G, k ) = mc 1(G, k )

mc (G, k ) , (5.7) where mc (G, k ) denotes the best cut value in [44]. It is evident that the larger ratio 0(G, k ) is, the better the approximation of Max -k-Cut is. From Table 16, we know that our proposed algorithms produce approximation solutions that surpass the reported ones in [44] especially for k ∈ { 3, 4, 5}. Specifically, we list the graph instances and the corresponding cut values that satisfy ratio 0 > 1 in Table 17. Table 16: Summary of ratio 0 in Eq. (5.7) for Max -k-Cut on G-set.

43 6 Numerical results for MinGCP 

This section presents numerical results of PEAF-ACMH, PEAF-MMH, ACMH, MMH, G-H and G-0.5H in solving six k-CUT problems (k = 2 , 3, 4, 5) in MinGCP: Normalized -

k-Cut (2.14), Cheeger -k-Cut (2.16), Min -k-Cut (2.12) MinMax -k-Cut (2.13), Ratio -

k-Cut (2.15) and Sparsest -k-Cut (2.17). Similar to the discussions in the last section for MaxGCP, we present four tables of numerical results and a summary table for each problem. 

## 6.1 Normalized-k-Cut 

Tables 23, 24, 25 and 26 display the numerical results for Normalized -k-Cut with 

k = 2 , 3, 4, 5. It can be easily observed that PEAF-ACMH, PEAF-MMH, ACMH and MMH outperform Gurobi on all graph instances except G70. Pairwise comparisons (see Table 27) of PEAF-MMH, MMH and ACMH reveal that integrating MMH with the parallel evolutionary framework or the auxiliary cut strategy yields improved solutions. 49 6.2 Cheeger-k-Cut 

Tables 28, 29, 30 and 31 present the numerical results for Cheeger -k-Cut with k ∈{2, 3, 4, 5}. It is evident that our four proposed algorithms—PEAF-ACMH, PEAF-MMH, ACMH, and MMH—consistently outperform Gurobi on all instances except for G70. As summarized in Table 32, PEAF-ACMH achieves the overall best performance among the four. Notably, the comparison between PEAF-MMH and MMH validates that the parallel evolutionary framework significantly enhances solution quality. 

Balance Analysis Numerical observations indicate that Cheeger -k-Cut promotes more balanced partitions compared to Normalized -k-Cut . Among the 140 best solutions iden-tified by PEAF-ACMH, Normalized -k-Cut yields 124 instances with smaller cut values, whereas Cheeger -k-Cut does so in only 3 instances. This suggests that Normalized -k-

Cut tends to prioritize minimizing the cut value (aligning more closely with Min -k-Cut ), thereby resulting in more skewed partitions than Cheeger -k-Cut .This behavior can be theoretically justified following the analysis in Section 5.2 for k = 2 .Consider a balanced partition {S, S c} where vol( S) ≃ vol( Sc). > 1

vol( S1) > 1 + αα cut( ∂S 1) for Cheeger-2-Cut ,

55 6.3 Min-k-Cut 

Tables 33, 34, 35 and 36 present the numerical results for Min -k-Cut with k ∈ { 2, 3, 4, 5}.Remarkably, all six algorithms yield identical solutions across all tested instances. Pairwise comparisons (see Table 37) further confirm that the performance differences between these algorithms are statistically negligible on G-set. Our four proposed algorithms consistently identify the optimal solutions within 20 seconds. The high efficiency observed here is consistent with the theoretical tractability of Min -k-Cut for a fixed k. The fact that our heuristic framework consistently identifies optimal solutions with such rapidity not only mirrors the problem’s polynomial-time nature but also serves as a testament to the high-fidelity search capability of our algorithms. 56 Table 33: Numerical results for  Min -2-Cut  on G-set.

61 6.4 MinMax-k-Cut 

Tables 38, 39, 40 and 41 display the numerical results for MinMax -k-Cut with k ∈{2, 3, 4, 5}. It is evident that our four proposed algorithms—PEAF-ACMH, PEAF-MMH, ACMH, and MMH—consistently match or exceed the performance of Gurobi across all in-stances. In the majority of cases, our framework identifies solutions identical to those of the exact solver, with PEAF-ACMH and ACMH yielding even better results for G58 and G63 at k = 5 . Furthermore, pairwise comparisons (see Table 42) indicate that the performance differences among the four variants are statistically negligible on G-set. A comparative analysis between PEAF-ACMH results for Min -k-Cut and MinMax -k-

Cut reveals a striking similarity: the absolute difference in their cut values is at most 4 (e.g., G52 in Tables 36 and 41). This minimal discrepancy, combined with the exceptionally low computational cost required to reach these solutions, provides compelling numerical evidence of the underlying structural affinity between the two problems. While the polynomial-time solvability of Min -k-Cut was established decades ago [26], the corresponding complexity for 

MinMax -k-Cut was only recently confirmed [13]. Our results are entirely consistent with this theoretical landscape, demonstrating that an efficient, high-fidelity heuristic framework can serve as a powerful tool for uncovering and validating the theoretical properties of com-binatorial k-CUT structures. 62 Table 38: Numerical results for  MinMax -2-Cut  on G-set.

67 6.5 Ratio-k-Cut 

Tables 43, 44, 45 and 46 present the numerical results for Ratio -k-Cut with k ∈ { 2, 3, 4, 5}.Notably, our proposed four algorithms—PEAF-ACMH, PEAF-MMH, ACMH, and MMH— consistently match or exceed the performance of Gurobi across all tested instances. Pairwise comparisons in Table 47 indicate that while the four variants perform similarly for k = 2 and 

3, a clear distinction emerges as k increases. For k = 4 and 5, PEAF-ACMH significantly outperforms its non-evolutionary counterpart, ACMH, thereby validating the effectiveness of the parallel evolutionary framework in further enhancing solution quality. Furthermore, a comparative analysis reveals that for certain graph instances (e.g., G22– G26 and G41–G47), the best cut values for Ratio -k-Cut are remarkably close to those of Min -k-Cut . These high-quality solutions are identified within a very short timeframe, suggesting a strong structural affinity between Ratio -k-Cut and Min -k-Cut on specific graph topologies. 68 Table 43: Numerical results for  Ratio -2-Cut  on G-set.

73 6.6 Sparsest-k-Cut 

Tables 48, 49, 50 and 51 display the numerical results for Sparsest -k-Cut with k ∈{2, 3, 4, 5}. Notably, our proposed algorithms—PEAF-ACMH, PEAF-MMH, ACMH, and MMH—consistently outperform Gurobi across all tested instances. As indicated by the pair-wise comparisons in Table 32, PEAF-ACMH achieves the superior performance among the four variants. 

Balance Analysis Numerical results suggest that Sparsest -k-Cut promotes more bal-anced partitions compared to Ratio -k-Cut . Among the 140 best solutions identified by PEAF-ACMH, Ratio -k-Cut yields smaller cut values in all instances, 112 of which are strictly smaller. This discrepancy confirms that Ratio -k-Cut tends to prioritize minimiz-ing the cut value (aligning more closely with Min -k-Cut ), thereby resulting in more skewed partitions than Sparsest -k-Cut .This behavior can be theoretically justified using an analysis similar to that in Section 5.2 for k = 2 . Assume a balanced partition {S, S c} where |S| ≃ | Sc| for both problems. Let 

cut( ∂S ) = α · 12|V |,

which leads to 

Sparsest-2-Cut ≃ 2α, 

Ratio-2-Cut ≃ 2α. 

If there exists a skewed cut {S1, S c

> 1

} satisfying |S1| ≪ | Sc

> 1

| ≃ | V | that is superior to {S, S c}

for both variants, then 

|S1| > 1

α cut( ∂S 1) for Sparsest-2-Cut ,

|S1| > 12α cut( ∂S 1) for Ratio-2-Cut .

This implies that Sparsest -k-Cut possesses a higher resistance to partition skewness, effec-tively maintaining better balance than Ratio -k-Cut , since 

1

α > 12α

holds for any α > 0.74 Table 48: Numerical results for  Sparsest -2-Cut  on G-set.

79 7 Conclusion and outlook 

We present a generalized Parallel Evolutionary Algorithm Framework (PEAF) for solv-ing a diverse suite of graph k-CUT problems. The framework integrates three synergized components: (1) a crossover stage with five operators ensuring that offspring inherit essen-tial structural traits from reference solutions; (2) a hierarchical mutation engine featuring the Multiple Mutation Heuristic (MMH) and its auxiliary cut-combined variant ACMH, which leverage nine local search operators to refine the computational complexity more efficient than 

O(k|V |); and (3) a selection mechanism that balances population quality and genetic diver-sity. Numerical experiments on nine widely studied k-CUT problems with k ∈ { 2, 3, 4, 5}

demonstrate that our proposed algorithms consistently match or exceeds the performance of 

Gurobi across the majority of G-set instances. The versatility of PEAF provides a robust foundation for addressing more complex graph partitioning variants. Potential extensions include: 1. Non-integer Weighted Graphs: For graphs with positive non-integer weights, the discrete bucket sorting mechanism can be adapted into an interval-based framework. By partitioning the range [−dmax , d max ] into N sub-intervals 

1 : 

(

bbound − 2

N bbound , b bound 

]

,

...

i :

(

bbound − 2iN bbound , b bound − 2i − 2

N bbound 

]

,

...

N :

[

−bbound , −bbound + 2

N bbound 

]

.

a doubly-linked list structure can manage vertices based on their move-gain values falling within specific intervals, maintaining efficient local search. 2. Signed Graphs: To handle signed graphs with mixed edge weights, the definitions of boundary |∂S p| and volume vol( Sp) must be generalized to incorporate both positive (w+

> uv

) and negative ( w−

> uv

) contributions [16]. Specifically, the boundary ∂S p and volume 

vol( Sp) can be redefined as shown in 

|∂S p| = ∑  

> u∈Sp, v ∈Scpand {u,v }∈ E

w+ 

> uv

and vol( Sp) = ∑

> u∈Sp

∑

> {u,v }∈ E

(w+ 

> uv

+ w−

> uv

),

respectively. Since the change in cut values during a single-transfer operation remains localized to the moved vertex and its neighbors, our bucket sorting framework can be extended to maintain stable move-gain matrices for signed structures. 3. Constrained k-Cut Problems: For variants with additional constraints, such as size balance limits |Sp| ≤ (1 + ν)|V |/k , ∀ p ∈ [k], ν > 0, [7], the framework can incorporate a ”Relax-and-Restore” strategy. This involves a feasible modification phase using a 80 step-by-step greedy migration to restore feasibility in the crossover and mutation phase. Specifically, for any solution I = {S1, . . . , S k}, let UI = {p ∈ [k] : |Sp| > (1 + ν)|V |/k }

be the set of unsatisfied constraints, then we iteratively move the vertex by 

I′ = I ◦ v → Sq with v, q ∈ arg opt  

> v∈∪ p∈UISp, q ∈[k]\UI

{f (I ◦ v → Sq)},

to make I′ feasible. Alternating between unconstrained relaxation and feasibility refine-ment allows the algorithm to iteratively converge toward high-quality feasible solutions.

# A Appendix: Proofs of Proposition 1 

To establish the computational complexities of the local search operators, we partition the proof into three critical components: the dynamics of move-gain updates, the efficiency of the bucket sorting data structure, and the incremental evaluation of the objective function. 

1. Move-Gain Update Dynamics Consider a single-transfer move Spv → v → Sqv (pv̸ =

qv). The update rules for the stable move-gain matrices ˜D and ̂ D are characterized by their temporal and spatial locality: 1. Vertex v: The gain values directly associated with the moved vertex v are updated according to new- ˜Dvi =

−̂ Dvi , if i = qv,̂

Dvi , otherwise .

2. Neighbors of v: For each neighbor u ∈ N (v), the gain for any single-transfer Spu →

u → Squ (pu̸ = qu) is adjusted via new- ˜Duq u = ˜Duq u + ˜ϕw uv , (A.1) 85 new-̂ Dui =̂ Dui +̂ ϕ{i}wuv , i ∈ { pu, q u, p v, q v}, (A.2) where the specific update terms ˜ϕ and ̂ ϕ{i} are detailed in Table 1. Since the update only affects v and its neighborhood N (v), at most k(dv + 1) elements are modified. Given that each element requires only O(1) time, the total update cost is bounded by O(kd v) ≤ O (kd max ).

2. Efficient Storage and Query via Bucket Sorting To facilitate the rapid identification of the primary search area D(I), we utilize a bucket sorting scheme (see Figures 4 and 5). • Structure: Each matrix D ∈ { ˜D,̂ D} is stored in k chief arrays. Each array Bq contains 

2dmax + 1 nodes, where each node Bqi acts as the head of a doubly-linked list for vertices with gain Dvq = dmax + 1 − i.• Search Area Retrieval: By maintaining pointers bq

and bq

to the first and last non-empty nodes in each Bq, the search area D(I) is reformulated as

(A.3) • Implementation: We implement these doubly-linked lists using two matrices M1, M2 ∈

Nk×| V |, where M1 

> iq

and M2 

> iq

store the predecessor and successor of vertex i within bucket 

Bq, respectively. As illustrated in Figures 4 and 5, moving a vertex within the bucket array entails a standard deletion and insertion sequence. These operations are exe-cuted through a fixed number of element reassignments in M1 and M2, resulting in a constant-time complexity of O(1) per move. Consequently, the total overhead for syn-chronizing the bucket structures is strictly dictated by the number of affected elements in the move-gain matrices, which remains bounded by O(kd max ).

3. Incremental Evaluation of the Objective Function Let c denote the marginal cost of computing the change in the objective function F (I) resulting from a move. In this framework, we avoid full re-evaluation. Taking Normalized -3-Cut as an example (Figure 1), we maintain the current boundary sizes cut (∂S) and volumes vol (S). When vertex e moves from S2 to S1, the new objective value is computed via 

F (I ◦ (e → S1)) = cut( ∂S 1) + ̂ D51 

vol( S1) + d5

+ cut( ∂S 2) + ̂ D52 

vol( S2) − d5

+ cut( ∂S 3) + ̂ D53 

vol( S3) .

This incremental approach requires only O(k) operations, regardless of the graph size |V |,thus c = O(k) is satisfied for all nine k-CUT problems in this paper. 86 4. Synthesis of Total Complexity Based on the above, the total complexity of the search operators is synthesized as follows: 1. Single-transfer ( ˜O1,̂ O1): The cost is the maximum of (a) searching D(I) (A.3), which is O(c|D(I)|), and (b) updating the gain matrices and pointers, which is O(kd max ). Since 

c = O(k), the total cost is: 

O(max {k|D(I)|, kd max }) = O(max {k|D(I)|, kd max }).

2. Double-transfer ( ˜O2,̂ O2): These operators explore move combinations within D(I)

and their neighborhoods, leading to at most |D(I)|dmax objective evaluations. Combined with the matrix update costs, the total complexity is: 

O(max {kd max , c |D(I)|dmax }) = O(kd max |D(I)|).

In sparse graphs, where |D(I)| ≪ | V |, these bounds demonstrate that the PEAF frame-work maintains high efficiency by confining the search and update operations to the localized boundaries of the partitions.
